	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Adoption</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg/250px-Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg/375px-Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg/500px-Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1178" data-file-height="1506" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Children_at_New_York_Foundling_cph.3a23917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sister_Irene" class="mw-redirect" title="Sister Irene">Sister Irene</a> of New York Foundling Hospital with children. Sister Irene is among the pioneers of modern adoption, establishing a system to board out children rather than institutionalize them.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Adoption</b> is a process whereby a person assumes the <a href="/wiki/Parenting" title="Parenting">parenting</a> of another, usually a child, from that person's biological or legal parent or parents. Legal adoptions permanently transfers all <a href="/wiki/Rights" title="Rights">rights</a> and responsibilities, along with <a href="/wiki/Filiation" title="Filiation">filiation</a>, from the biological parent or parents.
</p><p>In many jurisdictions the adopted person's full original birth certificate is cancelled and replaced with a fabricated post-adoption birth certificate which states that the child was born to the adoptive parents. This deception, when carried out, may continue with the adopted person for life and can be the cause for many well documented traumas experienced by the adopted person, including loss of identity, family history, culture, biological family (including not only biological parents but also siblings and extended family), family medical history and records, and increased risk of suicide, homelessness, incarceration, PTSD, depression, and anxiety.
</p><p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Guardianship" class="mw-redirect" title="Guardianship">guardianship</a> or other systems designed for the care of the young, adoption is intended to effect a permanent change in status and as such requires societal recognition, either through legal or religious sanction. Historically, some societies have enacted specific laws governing adoption; where others have tried to achieve adoption through less formal means, notably via contracts that specified <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> rights and <a href="/wiki/Parental_responsibility_(access_and_custody)" title="Parental responsibility (access and custody)">parental responsibilities</a> without an accompanying transfer of <a href="/wiki/Filiation" title="Filiation">filiation</a>. Modern systems of adoption, arising in the <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th century</a>, tend to be governed by comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Statutes" class="mw-redirect" title="Statutes">statutes</a> and regulations.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>Adoption for the well-born</dt></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg/170px-Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg/255px-Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg/340px-Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1648" data-file-height="2261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Traianus_Glyptothek_Munich_336.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> became emperor of Rome through adoption by the previous emperor <a href="/wiki/Nerva" title="Nerva">Nerva</a>, and was in turn succeeded by his own adopted son <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>. Adoption was a customary practice of the Roman Empire that enabled peaceful transitions of power</div></div></div>
<p>While the modern form of adoption emerged in the United States, forms of the practice appeared throughout history.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Code of Hammurabi</a>, for example, details the rights of adopters and the responsibilities of adopted individuals at length. The practice of <a href="/wiki/Adoption_in_ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in ancient Rome">adoption in ancient Rome</a> is well documented in the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Justinianus" class="mw-redirect" title="Codex Justinianus">Codex Justinianus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Markedly different from the modern period, ancient adoption practices put emphasis on the political and economic interests of the adopter,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Psychology_of_Adoption_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Psychology_of_Adoption-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> providing a legal tool that strengthened political ties between wealthy families and created male heirs to manage estates.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mary_Kathleen_Benet_1976,_page_14_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mary_Kathleen_Benet_1976,_page_14-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  The use of adoption by the aristocracy is well documented; many of Rome's emperors were adopted sons.<sup id="cite_ref-Mary_Kathleen_Benet_1976,_page_14_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mary_Kathleen_Benet_1976,_page_14-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adrogation" title="Adrogation">Adrogation</a> was a kind of Roman adoption which required the adrogator to be at least 60 years old.
</p><p>Infant adoption during Antiquity appears rare.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Psychology_of_Adoption_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Psychology_of_Adoption-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Child_abandonment" title="Child abandonment">Abandoned children</a> were often picked up for slavery<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and composed a significant percentage of the Empire's slave supply.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Roman legal records indicate that foundlings were occasionally taken in by families and raised as a son or daughter. Although not normally adopted under Roman Law, the children, called <i><a href="/wiki/Alumni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alumni">alumni</a></i>, were reared in an arrangement similar to guardianship, being considered the property of the father who abandoned them.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other ancient civilizations, notably <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">China</a>, used some form of adoption as well. Evidence suggests the goal of this practice was to ensure the continuity of cultural and religious practices; in contrast to the Western idea of extending family lines. In <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">ancient India</a>, secondary sonship, clearly denounced by the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> continued, in a limited and highly ritualistic form, so that an adopter might have the necessary <a href="/wiki/Funerary_rites" class="mw-redirect" title="Funerary rites">funerary rites</a> performed by a son.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> China had a similar idea of adoption with males adopted solely to perform the duties of <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancestor worship in China">ancestor worship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The practice of adopting the children of family members and close friends was common among the <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_culture" title="Polynesian culture">cultures of Polynesia</a> including <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Hawaii" title="Ancient Hawaii">Hawaii</a> where the custom was referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/H%C4%81nai" title="Hānai">hānai</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages_to_modern_period">Middle Ages to modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Middle Ages to modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>Adoption and commoners</dt></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg/220px-Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg/330px-Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg/440px-Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2536" data-file-height="2073" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller_003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>At the monastery gate</i> (<i>Am Klostertor</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Georg_Waldm%C3%BCller" title="Ferdinand Georg Waldmüller">Ferdinand Georg Waldmüller</a></div></div></div>
<p>The nobility of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> cultures that dominated Europe after the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> denounced the practice of adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> society, <a href="/wiki/Bloodline" class="mw-redirect" title="Bloodline">bloodlines</a> were paramount; a ruling dynasty lacking a "natural-born" <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> was replaced, a stark contrast to Roman traditions. The evolution of European law reflects this aversion to adoption. English <a href="/wiki/Common_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Law">Common Law</a>, for instance, did not permit adoption since it contradicted the customary rules of inheritance. In the same vein, France's <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a> made adoption difficult, requiring adopters to be over the age of 50, sterile, older than the adopted person by at least 15 years, and to have fostered the adoptee for at least six years.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  Some adoptions continued to occur, however, but became informal, based on ad hoc contracts. For example, in the year 737, in a charter from the town of <a href="/wiki/Lucca" title="Lucca">Lucca</a>, three adoptees were made heirs to an estate. Like other contemporary arrangements, the agreement stressed the responsibility of the adopted rather than adopter, focusing on the fact that, under the contract, the adoptive father was meant to be cared for in his old age; an idea that is similar to the conceptions of adoption under Roman law.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Europe's cultural makeover marked a period of significant innovation for adoption. Without support from the nobility, the practice gradually shifted toward abandoned children. Abandonment levels rose with the fall of the empire and many of the foundlings were left on the doorstep of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Kindness_of_Strangers_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Kindness_of_Strangers-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Initially, the clergy reacted by drafting rules to govern the exposing, selling, and rearing of abandoned children. The Church's innovation, however, was the practice of <a href="/wiki/Oblation" title="Oblation">oblation</a>, whereby children were dedicated to lay life within monastic institutions and reared within a <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a>. This created the first system in European history in which abandoned children did not have legal, social, or moral disadvantages. As a result, many of Europe's abandoned and orphaned children became <a href="/wiki/Alumni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alumni">alumni</a> of the Church, which in turn took the role of adopter. Oblation marks the beginning of a shift toward <a href="/wiki/Institutionalization" class="mw-redirect" title="Institutionalization">institutionalization</a>, eventually bringing about the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Foundling_hospital" title="Foundling hospital">foundling hospital</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orphanage" title="Orphanage">orphanage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Kindness_of_Strangers_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Kindness_of_Strangers-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the idea of institutional care gained acceptance, formal rules appeared about how to place children into families: boys could become apprenticed to an <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">artisan</a> and girls might be married off under the institution's authority.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  Institutions informally adopted out children as well, a mechanism treated as a way to obtain cheap <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">labor</a>, demonstrated by the fact that when the adopted died, their bodies were returned by the family to the institution for burial.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This system of <a href="/wiki/Apprenticeship" title="Apprenticeship">apprenticeship</a> and informal adoption extended into the 19th century, today seen as a transitional phase for adoption history. Under the direction of social welfare activists, orphan asylums began to promote adoptions based on sentiment rather than work; children were placed out under agreements to provide care for them as family members instead of under contracts for apprenticeship.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>  The growth of this model is believed to have contributed to the enactment of the first modern adoption law in 1851 by the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonwealth of Massachusetts">Commonwealth of Massachusetts</a>, unique in that it codified the ideal of the "best interests of the child."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Topic:_Timeline_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Topic:_Timeline-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>   Despite its intent, though, in practice, the system operated much the same as earlier incarnations. The experience of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Female_Asylum" title="Boston Female Asylum">Boston Female Asylum</a> (BFA) is a good example, which had up to 30% of its charges adopted out by 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  Officials of the BFA noted that, although the asylum promoted otherwise, adoptive parents did not distinguish between indenture and adoption; "We believe," the asylum officials said, "that often, when children of a younger age are taken to be adopted, the adoption is only another name for service."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_period">Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>Adopting to create a family</dt></dl>
<p>The next stage of adoption's evolution fell to the emerging nation of the United States. Rapid immigration and the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> resulted in unprecedented overcrowding of orphanages and foundling homes in the mid-nineteenth century. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Loring_Brace" title="Charles Loring Brace">Charles Loring Brace</a>, a Protestant minister became appalled by the legions of homeless <a href="/wiki/Waif" title="Waif">waifs</a> roaming the streets of New York City. Brace considered the abandoned youth, particularly Catholics, to be the most dangerous element challenging the city's order.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesLoringBrace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/38/CharlesLoringBrace.jpg/170px-CharlesLoringBrace.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/38/CharlesLoringBrace.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="175" data-file-height="221" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesLoringBrace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Loring_Brace" title="Charles Loring Brace">Charles Loring Brace</a></div></div></div>
<p>His solution was outlined in <i>The Best Method of Disposing of Our Pauper and Vagrant Children</i> (1859) which started the <a href="/wiki/Orphan_Train" title="Orphan Train">Orphan Train</a> movement. The orphan trains eventually shipped an estimated 200,000 children from the urban centers of the East to the nation's rural regions.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The children were generally <a href="/wiki/Indentured" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured">indentured</a>, rather than adopted, to families who took them in.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As in times past, some children were raised as members of the family while others were used as farm laborers and household servants.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
The sheer size of the displacement—the largest migration of children in history—and the degree of exploitation that occurred, gave rise to new agencies and a series of laws that promoted adoption arrangements rather than indenture. The hallmark of the period is <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>'s adoption law of 1917 which mandated investigation of all placements and limited record access to those involved in the adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the same period, the <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States" title="Progressivism in the United States">Progressive</a> movement swept the United States with a critical goal of ending the prevailing orphanage system. The culmination of such efforts came with the First White House Conference on the Care of Dependent Children called by President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> in 1909,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> where it was declared that the nuclear family represented "the highest and finest product of civilization" and was best able to serve as primary caretaker for the abandoned and orphaned.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Anti-institutional forces gathered momentum. As late as 1923, only two percent of children without parental care were in adoptive homes, with the balance in foster arrangements and orphanages. Less than forty years later, nearly one-third were in an adoptive home.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, the popularity of <a href="/wiki/Eugenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugenic">eugenic</a> ideas in America put up obstacles to the growth of adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> There were grave concerns about the genetic quality of illegitimate and indigent children, perhaps best exemplified by the influential writings of <a href="/wiki/Henry_H._Goddard" title="Henry H. Goddard">Henry H. Goddard</a> who protested against adopting children of unknown origin, saying,
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Now it happens that some people are interested in the welfare and high development of the human race; but leaving aside those exceptional people, all fathers and mothers are interested in the welfare of their own families. The dearest thing to the parental heart is to have the children marry well and rear a noble family. How short-sighted it is then for such a family to take into its midst a child whose pedigree is absolutely unknown; or, where, if it were partially known, the probabilities are strong that it would show poor and diseased stock, and that if a marriage should take place between that individual and any member of the family the offspring would be degenerates.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote><p> The period 1945 to 1974, the <a href="/wiki/Baby_Scoop_Era" title="Baby Scoop Era">baby scoop era</a>, saw rapid growth and acceptance of adoption as a means to build a family.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Illegitimate births rose three-fold after World War II, as <a href="/wiki/Sexual_revolution" title="Sexual revolution">sexual mores</a> changed. Simultaneously, the scientific community began to stress the dominance of nurture over genetics, chipping away at eugenic stigmas.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In this environment, adoption became the obvious solution for both unwed people and infertile couples.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Taken together, these trends resulted in a new American model for adoption. Following its Roman predecessor, Americans severed the rights of the original parents while making adopters the new parents in the eyes of the law. Two innovations were added: 1) adoption was meant to ensure the "best interests of the child;" the seeds of this idea can be traced to the first American adoption law in <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Topic:_Timeline_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Topic:_Timeline-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and 2) adoption became infused with secrecy, eventually resulting in the sealing of adoption and original birth records by 1945. The origin of the move toward secrecy began with Charles Loring Brace who introduced it to prevent children from the Orphan Trains from returning to or being reclaimed by their parents. Brace feared the impact of the parents' poverty, in general, and their Catholic religion, in particular, on the youth. This tradition of secrecy was carried on by the later Progressive reformers when drafting of American laws.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of adoptions in the United States peaked in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> It is uncertain what caused the subsequent decline. Likely contributing factors in the 1960s and 1970s include a decline in the fertility rate, associated with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">the pill</a>, the completion of legalization of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">artificial birth control</a> methods, the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Title_X" title="Title X">federal funding</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a> services available to the young and low income, and the legalization of abortion. In addition, the years of the late 1960s and early 1970s saw a dramatic change in society's view of <a href="/wiki/Illegitimacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Illegitimacy">illegitimacy</a> and in the legal rights<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> of those born outside of wedlock. In response, <a href="/wiki/Family_preservation" title="Family preservation">family preservation</a> efforts grew<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> so that few children born out of wedlock today are adopted. Ironically, adoption is far more visible and discussed in society today, yet it is less common.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The American model of adoption eventually proliferated globally. <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> established their first formal adoption law in 1926. The <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> passed its law in 1956. <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> made adoptees full members of the family in 1959. <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> enacted its first laws in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the Asian powers opened their orphanage systems to adoption, influenced as they were by Western ideas following colonial rule and military occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In France, local public institutions accredit candidates for adoption, who can then contact orphanages abroad, or ask for the support of NGOs. The system does not involve fees, but gives considerable power to social workers whose decisions may restrict adoption to standardized families (middle-age, medium to high income, heterosexual, Caucasian).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although adoption is today practiced globally, the United States has the largest number of children adopted per 100 live births. The table below provides a snapshot of Western adoption rates. Adoption in the United States still occurs at nearly three times those of its peers although the number of children awaiting adoption has held steady in recent years, hovering between 133,000 and 129,000 during the period 2002 to 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
