<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_adoption">Contemporary adoption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contemporary adoption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_of_adoption">Forms of adoption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Forms of adoption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Contemporary adoption practices can be open or closed.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Open_adoption" title="Open adoption">Open adoption</a> allows identifying information to be communicated between adoptive and biological parents and, perhaps, interaction between kin and the adopted person.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Open adoption can be an informal arrangement subject to termination by adoptive parents who have sole custody over the child. In some jurisdictions, the biological and adoptive parents may enter into a legally enforceable and binding agreement concerning visitation, exchange of information, or other interaction regarding the child.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> As of February 2009, 24 U.S. states allowed legally enforceable open adoption contract agreements to be included in the adoption finalization.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The practice of <a href="/wiki/Closed_adoption" title="Closed adoption">closed adoption</a> (aka confidential or secret adoption),<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> which has not been the norm for most of modern history,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> seals all identifying information, maintaining it as secret and preventing disclosure of the adoptive parents', biological kins', and adoptees' identities. Nevertheless, closed adoption may allow the transmittal of non-identifying information such as medical history and religious and ethnic background.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Today, as a result of <a href="/wiki/Safe_haven_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Safe haven law">safe haven laws</a> passed by some U.S. states, secret adoption is seeing renewed influence. In so-called "safe-haven" states, infants can be left, anonymously, at hospitals, fire departments, or police stations within a few days of birth, a practice criticized by some adoption advocacy organizations as being retrograde and dangerous.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="How_adoptions_originate">How adoptions originate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: How adoptions originate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg/220px-Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg/330px-Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg/440px-Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Entrance_to_the_New_York_Foundling_Home.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Foundling" class="mw-redirect" title="The New York Foundling">New York Foundling Home</a> is among North America's oldest adoption agencies</div></div></div>
<p>Adoptions can occur either between related family members, or unrelated individuals. Historically, most adoptions occurred within a family. The most recent data from the U.S. indicates about half of adoptions are currently between related individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> A common example of this is a "stepparent adoption", where the new partner of a parent may legally adopt a child from the parent's previous relationship. Intra-family adoption can also occur through surrender, as a result of parental death, or when the child cannot otherwise be cared for and a family member agrees to take over.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">Infertility</a> is the main reason parents seek to adopt children they are not related to. One study shows this accounted for 80% of unrelated infant adoptions and half of adoptions through foster care.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Estimates suggest that 11–24% of Americans who cannot conceive or carry to term attempt to build a family through adoption, and that the overall rate of ever-married American women who adopt is about 1.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Other reasons people adopt are numerous although not well documented. These may include wanting to cement a new family following divorce or death of one parent, compassion motivated by religious or philosophical conviction, to avoid contributing to <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a> out of the belief that it is more responsible to care for otherwise parent-less children than to reproduce, to ensure that inheritable diseases (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Tay%E2%80%93Sachs_disease" title="Tay–Sachs disease">Tay–Sachs disease</a>) are not passed on, and health concerns relating to pregnancy and childbirth. Although there are a range of possible reasons, the most recent study of experiences of women who adopt suggests they are most likely to be 40–44 years of age, currently married, have impaired fertility, and childless.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unrelated adoptions may occur through the following mechanisms:
</p>
<ul><li>Private <a href="/wiki/Domestic_adoption" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic adoption">domestic adoptions</a>: under this arrangement, charities and for-profit organizations act as intermediaries, bringing together prospective adoptive parents and families who want to place a child, all parties being residents of the same country. Alternatively, prospective adoptive parents sometimes avoid intermediaries and connect with women directly, often with a written contract; this is not permitted in some jurisdictions. Private domestic adoption accounts for a significant portion of all adoptions; in the United States, for example, nearly 45% of adoptions are estimated to have been arranged privately.<sup id="cite_ref-childwelfare.gov_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-childwelfare.gov-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg/220px-%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg/330px-%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg/440px-%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22%D0%9D%D0%B0%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%96_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D1%96%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9%22_%D0%B2_%D0%A3%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%97%D0%BD%D1%96.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children associated with <i>Hope and Homes for Children</i>, a foster care program in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a></div></div></div></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Foster_care_adoption" class="mw-redirect" title="Foster care adoption">Foster care adoption</a>: this is a type of domestic adoption where a child is initially placed in public care. Many times the foster parents take on the adoption when the children become legally free. Its importance as an avenue for adoption varies by country. Of the 127,500 adoptions in the U.S. in 2000<sup id="cite_ref-childwelfare.gov_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-childwelfare.gov-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> about 51,000 or 40% were through the foster care system.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_adoption" title="International adoption">International adoption</a>: involves the placing of a child for adoption outside that child's country of birth. This can occur through both public and private agencies. In some countries, such as Sweden, these adoptions account for the majority of cases (see above Table). The U.S. example, however, indicates there is wide variation by country since adoptions from abroad account for less than 15% of its cases.<sup id="cite_ref-childwelfare.gov_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-childwelfare.gov-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> More than 60,000 Russian children have been adopted in the United States since 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and a similar number of Chinese children were adopted from 1995 to 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The laws of different countries vary in their willingness to allow international adoptions. Recognizing the difficulties and challenges associated with international adoption, and in an effort to protect those involved from the corruption and exploitation which sometimes accompanies it, the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conference_on_Private_International_Law" title="Hague Conference on Private International Law">Hague Conference on Private International Law</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Adoption_Convention" title="Hague Adoption Convention">Hague Adoption Convention</a>, which came into force on 1 May 1995 and has been ratified by 85 countries as of November 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Embryo_donation" title="Embryo donation">Embryo adoption</a>: based on the donation of embryos remaining after one couple's <a href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilization" class="mw-redirect" title="In vitro fertilization">in vitro fertilization</a> treatments have been completed; embryos are given to another individual or couple, followed by the placement of those embryos into the recipient woman's uterus, to facilitate pregnancy and childbirth. In the United States, embryo adoption is governed by property law rather than by the court systems, in contrast to traditional adoption.</li>
<li>Common law adoption: this is an adoption which has not been recognized beforehand by the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Court of law">courts</a>, but where a parent, without resorting to any formal <a href="/wiki/Legal_process" title="Legal process">legal process</a>, leaves his or her children with a friend or relative for an extended period of time.<sup id="cite_ref-GVB_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GVB-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JG_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JG-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>  At the end of a designated term of (voluntary) <a href="/wiki/Co-habitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Co-habitation">co-habitation</a>, as witnessed by the public, the adoption is then considered binding, in some courts of law, even though not initially sanctioned by the court. The particular terms of a common-law adoption are defined by each legal <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a>. For example, the US state of California recognizes <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> relationships after co-habitation of 2 years. The practice is called "private fostering" in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-SEC_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEC-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disruption_and_dissolution">Disruption and dissolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Disruption and dissolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Disruption_(adoption)" title="Disruption (adoption)">Disruption (adoption)</a></div>
<p>Although adoption is often described as forming a "forever" family, the relationship can be ended at any time.  The legal termination of an adoption is called <a href="/wiki/Disruption_(adoption)" title="Disruption (adoption)">disruption</a>.  In U.S. terminology, adoptions are <i>disrupted</i> if they are ended before being finalized, and they are <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_(law)" title="Dissolution (law)"><i>dissolved</i></a> if the relationship is ended afterwards.  It may also be called a <b>failed adoption</b>.  After legal finalization, the disruption process is usually initiated by adoptive parents via a <a href="/wiki/Court" title="Court">court</a> <a href="/wiki/Petition" title="Petition">petition</a> and is analogous to <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce proceedings</a>. It is a legal avenue unique to adoptive parents as disruption/dissolution does not apply to biological kin, although biological family members can be <a href="/wiki/Disownment" title="Disownment">disowned</a> or <a href="/wiki/Child_abandonment" title="Child abandonment">abandoned</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adoption_Disruption_and_Dissolution_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adoption_Disruption_and_Dissolution-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Ad hoc</i> studies, performed in the U.S., however, suggest that between 10 and 25 percent of adoptions through the child welfare system (e.g., excluding babies adopted from other countries or stepparents adopting their stepchildren) disrupt before they are legally finalized and from 1 to 10 percent are dissolved after legal finalization. The wide range of values reflects the paucity of information on the subject and demographic factors such as age; it is known that teenagers are more prone to having their adoptions disrupted than young children.<sup id="cite_ref-Adoption_Disruption_and_Dissolution_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adoption_Disruption_and_Dissolution-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption_by_same-sex_couples">Adoption by same-sex couples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Adoption by same-sex couples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_adoption" title="LGBT adoption">LGBT adoption</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg/300px-World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg/450px-World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg/600px-World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="434" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_same-sex_adoption_laws.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Legal status of adoption by same-sex couples around the world: <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #800080; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Joint adoption allowed</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ba75ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Second-parent_adoption" class="mw-redirect" title="Second-parent adoption">Second-parent adoption</a> allowed</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e0e0e0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;No laws allowing adoption by same-sex couples</div></div></div></div>
<p>Joint adoption by same-sex couples is legal in 26 countries, and in various sub-national territories. LGBT adoption may also be in the form of step-child adoption, wherein one partner in a same-sex couple adopts the biological child of the other partner.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parenting_of_adoptees">Parenting of adoptees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Parenting of adoptees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parenting">Parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The biological relationship between a parent and child is important, and the separation of the two has led to concerns about adoption. The traditional view of adoptive parenting received empirical support from a <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> study of 6,000 adoptive, step, and foster families in the United States and South Africa from 1968 to 1985; the study indicated that food expenditures in households with mothers of non-biological children (when controlled for income, household size, hours worked, age, etc.) were significantly less for adoptees; step-children, and foster children, causing the researchers to speculate that, instinctively, people are less interested in sustaining the genetic lines of others.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>  This theory is supported in another more qualitative study where in adoptive relationships marked by sameness in likes, personality, and appearance, both adult adoptees and adoptive parents report being happier with the adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other studies provide evidence that adoptive relationships can form along other lines. A study evaluating the level of parental investment indicates strength in adoptive families, suggesting that parents who adopt invest more time in their children than other parents and concludes, "...adoptive parents enrich their children's lives to compensate for the lack of biological ties and the extra challenges of adoption."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Another recent study found that adoptive families invested more heavily in their adopted children, for example, by providing further education and financial support. Noting that adoptees seemed to be more likely to experience problems such as drug addiction, the study speculated that adoptive parents might invest more in adoptees not because they favor them, but because they are more likely than genetic children to need the help.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Psychologists' findings regarding the importance of early mother-infant bonding created some concern about whether parents who adopt older infants or toddlers after birth have missed some crucial period for the child's development. However, research on <a href="/wiki/The_Mental_and_Social_Life_of_Babies" title="The Mental and Social Life of Babies">The Mental and Social Life of Babies</a> suggested that the "parent-infant system," rather than a bond between biologically related individuals, is an evolved fit between innate behavior patterns of all human infants and equally evolved responses of human adults to those infant behaviors. Thus nature "ensures some initial flexibility with respect to the particular adults who take on the parental role."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond the foundational issues, the unique questions posed for adoptive parents are varied. They include how to respond to stereotypes, answering questions about heritage, and how best to maintain connections with biological kin when in an open adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  One author suggests a common question adoptive parents have is: "Will we love the child even though he/she is not our biological child?"<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  A specific concern for many parents is accommodating an adoptee in the classroom.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Familiar lessons like "draw your <a href="/wiki/Family_tree" title="Family tree">family tree</a>" or "trace your eye color back through your parents and grandparents to see where your genes come from" could be hurtful to children who were adopted and do not know this biological information. Numerous suggestions have been made to substitute new lessons, e.g., focusing on "family orchards."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Adopting older children presents other parenting issues.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Some children from foster care have histories of maltreatment, such as physical and psychological neglect, physical abuse, and sexual abuse, and are at risk of developing psychiatric problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Gauthier,_Stollak,_Messe,_&amp;_Arnoff,_(1996)_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gauthier,_Stollak,_Messe,_&amp;_Arnoff,_(1996)-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Malinosky-Rummell_&amp;_Hansen,_(1993)_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malinosky-Rummell_&amp;_Hansen,_(1993)-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Such children are at risk of developing a <a href="/wiki/Attachment_Theory#Attachment_patterns" class="mw-redirect" title="Attachment Theory">disorganized attachment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lyons-Ruth_&amp;_Jacobvitz,_(1999)_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons-Ruth_&amp;_Jacobvitz,_(1999)-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solomon_&amp;_George,_(1999)_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon_&amp;_George,_(1999)-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Main_&amp;_Hesse,_(1990)_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Main_&amp;_Hesse,_(1990)-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Studies by Cicchetti et al. (1990, 1995) found that 80% of abused and maltreated infants in their sample exhibited disorganized attachment styles.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson,_V.,_Cicchetti,_D.,_Barnett,_D.,_&amp;_Bruanwald,_K.,_(1995)_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson,_V.,_Cicchetti,_D.,_Barnett,_D.,_&amp;_Bruanwald,_K.,_(1995)-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cicchetti,_D.,_Cummings,_EM,_Greengerg,_MT,_&amp;_Marvin,_RS._(1990)_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cicchetti,_D.,_Cummings,_EM,_Greengerg,_MT,_&amp;_Marvin,_RS._(1990)-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
Disorganized attachment is associated with a number of developmental problems, including dissociative symptoms,<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson,_E.A._(1988)_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson,_E.A._(1988)-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> as well as depressive, anxiety, and acting-out symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-Lyons-Ruth,_K_(1996)_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons-Ruth,_K_(1996)-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lyons-Ruth,_Alpern,_&amp;_Pepacholi,_(1993)_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons-Ruth,_Alpern,_&amp;_Pepacholi,_(1993)-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> "Attachment is an active process- it can be secure or insecure, maladaptive or productive."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>  In the UK some adoptions fail because the adoptive parents do not get sufficient support to deal with difficult, traumatized children.  This is a <a href="/wiki/False_economy" title="False economy">false economy</a> as local authority care for these children is extremely expensive.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerning developmental milestones, studies from the Colorado Adoption Project examined <a href="/wiki/Heritability" title="Heritability">genetic influences</a> on adoptee maturation, concluding that cognitive abilities of adoptees reflect those of their adoptive parents in early childhood but show little similarity by adolescence, resembling instead those of their biological parents and to the same extent as peers in non-adoptive families.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_1997_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_1997-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similar mechanisms appear to be at work in the physical development of adoptees. Danish and American researchers conducting studies on the genetic contribution to <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> found correlations between an adoptee's weight class and his biological parents' BMI while finding no relationship with the adoptive family environment. Moreover, about one-half of inter-individual differences were due to individual non-shared influences.<sup id="cite_ref-The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-International_journal_of_obesity_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_journal_of_obesity-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These differences in development appear to play out in the way young adoptees deal with major life events. In the case of parental divorce, adoptees have been found to respond differently from children who have not been adopted. While the general population experienced more behavioral problems, substance use, lower school achievement, and impaired social competence after parental divorce, the adoptee population appeared to be unaffected in terms of their outside relationships, specifically in their school or social abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-American_Psychological_Association_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American_Psychological_Association-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_the_original_parents">Effects on the original parents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Effects on the original parents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several factors affect the decision to release or raise the child. White adolescents tend to give up their babies to non-relatives, whereas black adolescents are more likely to receive support from their own community in raising the child and also in the form of informal adoption by relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Studies by Leynes and by Festinger and Young, Berkman, and Rehr found that for pregnant adolescents, the decision to release the child for adoption depended on the attitude toward adoption held by the adolescent's mother.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Another study found that pregnant adolescents whose mothers had a higher level of education were more likely to release their babies for adoption. Research suggests that women who choose to release their babies for adoption are more likely to be younger, enrolled in school, and have lived in a two-parent household at age 10, than those who kept and raised their babies.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is limited research on the consequences of adoption for the original parents, and the findings have been mixed. One study found that those who released their babies for adoption were less comfortable with their decision than those who kept their babies. However, levels of comfort over both groups were high, and those who released their child were similar to those who kept their child in ratings of life satisfaction, relationship satisfaction, and positive future outlook for schooling, employment, finances, and marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>  Subsequent research found that adolescent mothers who chose to release their babies for adoption were more likely to experience feelings of sorrow and regret over their decision than those who kept their babies. However, these feelings decreased significantly from one year after birth to the end of the second year.<sup id="cite_ref-Donnelly,_B.W._&amp;_Voydanoff,_P_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donnelly,_B.W._&amp;_Voydanoff,_P-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More recent research found that in a sample of mothers who had released their children for adoption four to 12 years prior, every participant had frequent thoughts of their lost child. For most, thoughts were both negative and positive in that they produced both feelings of sadness and joy. Those who experienced the greatest portion of positive thoughts were those who had open, rather than closed or time-limited mediated adoptions.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In another study that compared mothers who released their children to those who raised them, mothers who released their children were more likely to delay their next pregnancy, to delay marriage, and to complete job training. However, both groups reached lower levels of education than their peers who were never pregnant.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>  Another study found similar consequences for choosing to release a child for adoption. Adolescent mothers who released their children were more likely to reach a higher level of education and to be employed than those who kept their children. They also waited longer before having their next child.<sup id="cite_ref-Donnelly,_B.W._&amp;_Voydanoff,_P_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donnelly,_B.W._&amp;_Voydanoff,_P-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Most of the research that exists on adoption effects on the birth parents was conducted with samples of adolescents, or with women who were adolescents when carrying their babies—little data exists for birth parents from other populations. Furthermore, there is a lack of longitudinal data that may elucidate long-term social and psychological consequences for birth parents who choose to place their children for adoption.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_adoptees">Development of adoptees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Development of adoptees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Previous research on adoption has led to assumptions that indicate that there is a heightened risk in terms of psychological development and social relationships for adoptees. Yet, such assumptions have been clarified as flawed due to methodological failures. But more recent studies have been supportive in indicating more accurate information and results about the similarities, differences and overall lifestyles of adoptees.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence about the development of adoptees can be supported in newer studies. It can be said that adoptees, in some respect, tend to develop differently from the general population. This can be seen in many aspects of life, but usually can be found as a greater risk around the time of adolescence. For example, it has been found that many adoptees experience difficulty in establishing a sense of identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Beauchesne_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beauchesne-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many ways in which the concept of identity can be defined. It is true in all cases that identity construction is an ongoing process of development, change and maintenance of identifying with the self. Research has shown that adolescence is a time of identity progression rather than regression.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> One's identity tends to lack stability in the beginning years of life but gains a more stable sense in later periods of childhood and adolescence. Typically associated with a time of experimentation, there are endless factors that go into the construction of one's identity. As well as being many factors, there are many types of identities one can associate with. Some categories of identity include gender, sexuality, class, racial and religious, etc. For transracial and <a href="/wiki/International_adoption" title="International adoption">international</a> adoptees, tension is generally found in the categories of racial, ethnic and national identification. Because of this, the strength and functionality of family relationships play a huge role in its development and outcome of identity construction. Transracial and transnational adoptees tend to develop feelings of a lack of acceptance because of such racial, ethnic, and cultural differences. Therefore, exposing transracial and transnational adoptees to their "cultures of origin" is important in order to better develop a sense of identity and appreciation for cultural diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Orphan_Sunday_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orphan_Sunday-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Identity construction and reconstruction for transnational adoptees the instant they are adopted. For example, based upon specific laws and regulations of the United States, the Child Citizen Act of 2000 makes sure to grant immediate U.S. citizenship to adoptees.<sup id="cite_ref-Orphan_Sunday_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orphan_Sunday-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Identity is defined both by what one is and what one is not. Adoptees born into one family lose an identity and then borrow one from the adopting family.
The formation of identity is a complicated process and there are many factors that affect its outcome. From a perspective of looking at issues in adoption circumstances, the people involved and affected by adoption (the biological parent, the adoptive parent and the adoptee) can be known as the "triad members and state".
Adoption may threaten triad members' sense of identity. Triad members often express feelings related to confused identity and identity crises because of differences between the triad relationships.
Adoption, for some, precludes a complete or integrated sense of self. Triad members may experience themselves as incomplete, deficient, or unfinished. They state that they lack feelings of well-being, integration, or solidity associated with a fully developed identity.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Family plays a vital role in identity formation. This is not only true in childhood but also in adolescence. Identity (gender/sexual/ethnic/religious/family) is still forming during adolescence and family holds a vital key to this.
The research seems to be unanimous; a stable, secure, loving, honest and supportive family in which all members feel safe to explore their identity is necessary for the formation of a sound identity. Transracial and <a href="/wiki/International_adoption" title="International adoption">International</a> adoptions are some factors that play a significant role in the identity construction of adoptees. Many tensions arise from relationships built between the adoptee(s) and their family. These include being "different" from the parent(s), developing a positive racial identity, and dealing with racial/ethnic discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> It has been found that multicultural and transnational youth tend to identify with their parents origin of culture and ethnicity rather than their residing location, yet it is sometimes hard to balance an identity between the two because school environments tend to lack diversity and acknowledgment regarding such topics.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> These tensions also tend to create questions for the adoptee, as well as the family, to contemplate. Some common questions include what will happen if the family is more naïve to the ways of socially constructed life? Will tensions arise if this is the case? What if the very people that are supposed to be modeling a sound identity are in fact riddled with insecurities? Ginni Snodgrass answers these questions in the following way.
The secrecy in an adoptive family and the denial that the adoptive family is different builds dysfunction into it. "...&#160;social workers and insecure adoptive parents have structured a family relationship that is based on dishonesty, evasions and exploitation. To believe that good relationships will develop on such a foundation is psychologically unsound" (Lawrence). Secrecy erects barriers to forming a healthy identity.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The research says that the dysfunction, untruths and evasiveness that can be present in adoptive families not only makes identity formation impossible, but also directly works against it. What effect on identity formation is present if the adoptee knows they are adopted but has no information about their biological parents? Silverstein and Kaplan's research states that adoptees lacking medical, genetic, religious, and historical information are plagued by questions such as "Who am I?" "Why was I born?" "What is my purpose?" This lack of identity may lead adoptees, particularly in adolescent years, to seek out ways to belong in a more extreme fashion than many of their non-adopted peers. Adolescent adoptees are overrepresented among those who join sub-cultures, run away, become pregnant, or totally reject their families.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerning developmental milestones, studies from the Colorado Adoption Project examined <a href="/wiki/Heritability" title="Heritability">genetic influences</a> on adoptee maturation, concluding that cognitive abilities of adoptees reflect those of their adoptive parents in early childhood but show little similarity by adolescence, resembling instead those of their biological parents and to the same extent as peers in non-adoptive families.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_1997_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_1997-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similar mechanisms appear to be at work in the physical development of adoptees. Danish and American researchers conducting studies on the genetic contribution to <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> found correlations between an adoptee's weight class and his biological parents' BMI while finding no relationship with the adoptive family environment. Moreover, about one-half of inter-individual differences were due to individual non-shared influences.<sup id="cite_ref-The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-International_journal_of_obesity_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_journal_of_obesity-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These differences in development appear to play out in the way young adoptees deal with major life events. In the case of parental divorce, adoptees have been found to respond differently from children who have not been adopted. While the general population experienced more behavioral problems, substance use, lower school achievement, and impaired social competence after parental divorce, the adoptee population appeared to be unaffected in terms of their outside relationships, specifically in their school or social abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-American_Psychological_Association_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American_Psychological_Association-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The adoptee population does, however, seem to be more at risk for certain behavioral issues. Researchers from the University of Minnesota studied adolescents who had been adopted and found that adoptees were twice as likely as non-adopted people to suffer from <a href="/wiki/Oppositional_defiant_disorder" title="Oppositional defiant disorder">oppositional defiant disorder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Attention-deficit/hyperactivity_disorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder">attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder</a> (with an 8% rate in the general population).<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Suicide risks were also significantly greater than the general population. Swedish researchers found both international and domestic adoptees undertook suicide at much higher rates than non-adopted peers; with international adoptees and female international adoptees, in particular, at highest risk.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, work on adult adoptees has found that the additional risks faced by adoptees are largely confined to adolescence. Young adult adoptees were shown to be alike with adults from biological families and scored better than adults raised in alternative family types including single parent and step-families.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>  Moreover, while adult adoptees showed more variability than their non-adopted peers on a range of psychosocial measures, adult adoptees exhibited more similarities than differences with adults who had not been adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> There have been many cases of remediation or the reversibility of early trauma. For example, in one of the earliest studies conducted, Professor Goldfarb in England concluded that some children adjust well socially and emotionally despite their negative experiences of institutional deprivation in early childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Other researchers also found that prolonged institutionalization does not necessarily lead to emotional problems or character defects in all children. This suggests that there will always be some children who fare well, who are resilient, regardless of their experiences in early childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, much of the research on psychological outcomes for adoptees draws from clinical populations. This suggests that conclusions such that adoptees are more likely to have behavioral problems such as ODD and ADHD may be biased. Since the proportion of adoptees that seek mental health treatment is small, psychological outcomes for adoptees compared to those for the general population are more similar than some researchers propose.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_perception_of_adoption">Public perception of adoption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Public perception of adoption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg/280px-Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg/420px-Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg/560px-Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_of_Green_Gables.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Actors at the <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Green_Gables" title="Anne of Green Gables">Anne of Green Gables</a> Museum on <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>, Canada. Since its first publication in 1908, the story of the orphaned Anne, and how the Cuthberts took her in, has been widely popular in the English-speaking world and, later, Japan.</div></div></div>
<p>In Western culture, many see that the common image of a family being that of a heterosexual couple with biological children. This idea places alternative family forms outside the norm. As a consequence, research indicates, disparaging views of adoptive families exist, along with doubts concerning the strength of their family bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most recent adoption attitudes survey completed by the Evan Donaldson Institute provides further evidence of this stigma. Nearly one-third of the surveyed population believed adoptees are less-well adjusted, more prone to medical issues, and predisposed to drug and alcohol problems. Additionally, 40–45% thought adoptees were more likely to have behavior problems and trouble at school. In contrast, the same study indicated adoptive parents were viewed favorably, with nearly 90% describing them as "lucky, advantaged, and unselfish."<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of people state that their primary source of information about adoption comes from friends and family and the news media. Nevertheless, most people report the media provides them a favorable view of adoption; 72% indicated receiving positive impressions.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> There is, however, still substantial criticism of the media's adoption coverage. Some adoption blogs, for example, criticized <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Robinsons" title="Meet the Robinsons">Meet the Robinsons</a></i> for using outdated orphanage imagery<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> as did advocacy non-profit The Evan B. Donaldson Adoption Institute.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The stigmas associated with adoption are amplified for children in <a href="/wiki/Foster_care" title="Foster care">foster care</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Negative perceptions result in the belief that such children are so troubled it would be impossible to adopt them and create "normal" families.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>  A 2004 report from the Pew Commission on Children in Foster Care has shown that the number of children waiting in foster care doubled since the 1980s and now remains steady at about a half-million a year."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_and_reunion_trends">Reform and reunion trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reform and reunion trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adoption_roots_with_tree.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Adoption_roots_with_tree.png/170px-Adoption_roots_with_tree.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Adoption_roots_with_tree.png/255px-Adoption_roots_with_tree.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Adoption_roots_with_tree.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adoption_roots_with_tree.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Open Records emblem used in Adoptee Rights Protest, New Orleans, 2008, artist: D. Martin</div></div></div>
<p>Adoption practices have changed significantly over the course of the 20th century, with each new movement labeled, in some way, as reform.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in the 1970s, efforts to improve adoption became associated with opening records and encouraging family preservation. These ideas arose from suggestions that the secrecy inherent in modern adoption may influence the process of forming an <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">identity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-primal-page.com_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primal-page.com-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> create confusion regarding <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> and provide little in the way of medical history.
</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/Family_preservation" title="Family preservation">Family preservation</a>:</b> As concerns over illegitimacy began to decline in the early 1970s, social-welfare agencies began to emphasize that, if possible, mothers and children should be kept together.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In the U.S., this was clearly illustrated by the shift in policy of the New York Foundling Home, an adoption-institution that is among the country's oldest and one that had pioneered sealed records. It established three new principles including "to prevent placements of children...," reflecting the belief that children would be better served by staying with their biological families, a striking shift in policy that remains in force today.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Open records:</b> Movements to unseal adoption records for adopted citizens proliferated along with increased acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Illegitimacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Illegitimacy">illegitimacy</a>. In the United States, Jean Paton founded Orphan Voyage in 1954, and Florence Fisher founded the Adoptees' Liberty Movement Association (ALMA) in 1971, calling sealed records "an affront to human dignity.".<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> While in 1975, Emma May Vilardi created the first mutual-consent registry, the <a href="/wiki/International_Soundex_Reunion_Registry" title="International Soundex Reunion Registry">International Soundex Reunion Registry</a> (ISRR), allowing those separated by adoption to locate one another.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> and Lee Campbell and other birthmothers established CUB (<a href="/wiki/Concerned_United_Birthparents" title="Concerned United Birthparents">Concerned United Birthparents</a>). Similar ideas were taking hold globally with grass-roots organizations like Parent Finders in Canada and Jigsaw in Australia. In 1975, England and Wales opened records on moral grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1979, representatives of 32 organizations from 33 states, Canada and Mexico gathered in Washington, DC to establish the <a href="/wiki/American_Adoption_Congress" title="American Adoption Congress">American Adoption Congress</a> (AAC) passing a unanimous resolution: "Open Records complete with all identifying information for all members of the adoption triad, birthparents, adoptive parents and adoptee at the adoptee's <a href="/wiki/Age_of_majority" title="Age of majority">age of majority</a> (18 or 19, depending on state) or earlier if all members of the triad agree."<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Later years saw the evolution of more militant organizations such as <a href="/wiki/Bastard_Nation" title="Bastard Nation">Bastard Nation</a> (founded in 1996), groups that helped overturn sealed records in Alabama, Delaware, New Hampshire, Oregon, Tennessee, and Maine.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Simultaneously, groups such as Origins USA (founded in 1997) started to actively speak about family preservation and the rights of mothers.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>  The intellectual tone of these recent reform movements was influenced by the publishing of <i>The Primal Wound</i> by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nancy_Verrier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nancy Verrier (page does not exist)">Nancy Verrier</a>. "Primal wound" is described as the "devastation which the infant feels because of separation from its birth mother. It is the deep and consequential feeling of abandonment which the baby adoptee feels after the adoption and which may continue for the rest of his life."<sup id="cite_ref-primal-page.com_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primal-page.com-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reunion">Reunion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reunion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Estimates for the extent of search behavior by adoptees have proven elusive; studies show significant variation.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> In part, the problem stems from the small adoptee population which makes random surveying difficult, if not impossible.
</p><p>Nevertheless, some indication of the level of search interest by adoptees can be gleaned from the case of England and Wales which opened adoptees' birth records in 1975. The UK Office for National Statistics has projected that 33% of all adoptees would eventually request a copy of their original birth records, exceeding original forecasts made in 1975 when it was believed that only a small fraction of the adoptee population would request their records. The projection is known to underestimate the true search rate, however, since many adoptees of the era get their birth records by other means.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The research literature states adoptees give four reasons for desiring reunion: 1) they wish for a more complete genealogy, 2) they are curious about events leading to their conception, birth, and relinquishment, 3) they hope to pass on information to their children, and 4) they have a need for a detailed biological background, including medical information. It is speculated by adoption researchers, however, that the reasons given are incomplete: although such information could be communicated by a third-party, interviews with adoptees, who sought reunion, found they expressed a need to actually meet biological relations.<sup id="cite_ref-cite_jstor&#124;353920_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cite_jstor|353920-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It appears the desire for reunion is linked to the adoptee's interaction with and acceptance within the community. Internally focused theories suggest some adoptees possess ambiguities in their sense of self, impairing their ability to present a consistent identity. Reunion helps resolve the lack of self-knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Externally focused theories, in contrast, suggest that reunion is a way for adoptees to overcome social stigma. First proposed by Goffman, the theory has four parts: 1) adoptees perceive the absence of biological ties as distinguishing their adoptive family from others, 2) this understanding is strengthened by experiences where non-adoptees suggest adoptive ties are weaker than blood ties, 3) together, these factors engender, in some adoptees, a sense of social exclusion, and 4) these adoptees react by searching for a blood tie that reinforces their membership in the community. The externally focused rationale for reunion suggests adoptees may be well adjusted and happy within their adoptive families, but will search as an attempt to resolve experiences of social stigma.<sup id="cite_ref-cite_jstor&#124;353920_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cite_jstor|353920-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some adoptees reject the idea of reunion. It is unclear, though, what differentiates adoptees who search from those who do not. One paper summarizes the research, stating, "...attempts to draw distinctions between the searcher and non-searcher are no more conclusive or generalizable than attempts to substantiate...differences between adoptees and nonadoptees."<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In sum, reunions can bring a variety of issues for adoptees and parents. Nevertheless, most reunion results appear to be positive. In the largest study to date (based on the responses of 1,007 adoptees and relinquishing parents), 90% responded that reunion was a beneficial experience. This does not, however, imply ongoing relationships were formed between adoptee and parent nor that this was the goal.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The book "Adoption Detective: Memoir of an Adopted Child" by Judith and Martin Land provides insight into the mind of an adoptee from childhood through to adulthood and the emotions invoked when reunification with their birth mothers is desired.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_adoption_practices">Controversial adoption practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Controversial adoption practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Adoption_fraud" title="Adoption fraud">Adoption fraud</a></div>
<p>Reform and family preservation efforts have also been strongly associated with the perceived misuse of adoption. In some cases, parents' rights have been terminated when their ethnic or socio-economic group has been deemed unfit by society. Some of these practices were generally accepted but have later been considered abusive; others were uncontroversially reprehensible.
</p><p>Forced adoption based on ethnicity occurred during World War II. In German occupied Poland, it is estimated that 200,000 Polish children with purportedly Aryan traits were <a href="/wiki/Kidnapping_of_Polish_children_by_Nazi_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Kidnapping of Polish children by Nazi Germany">removed from their families</a> and given to German or Austrian couples,<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and only 25,000 returned to their families after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Stolen_Generation" class="mw-redirect" title="Stolen Generation">Stolen Generation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal Peoples">Aboriginal people</a> in Australia were affected by similar policies,<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> as were <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans in the United States</a><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These practices have become significant social and political issues in recent years, and in many cases the policies have changed.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The United States, for example, now has the 1978 <a href="/wiki/Indian_Child_Welfare_Act" title="Indian Child Welfare Act">Indian Child Welfare Act</a>, which allows the tribe and family of a Native American child to be involved in adoption decisions, with preference being given to adoption within the child's tribe.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 1950s through the 1970s, a period called the <a href="/wiki/Baby_Scoop_Era" title="Baby Scoop Era">baby scoop era</a>, adoption practices that involved coercion were directed against unwed mothers, as described for the US in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Girls_Who_Went_Away" title="The Girls Who Went Away">The Girls Who Went Away</a>.</i>
</p><p>More recently the <a href="/wiki/National_Reorganization_Process" title="National Reorganization Process">military dictatorship in Argentina from 1976 to 1983</a> is known to have given hundreds of babies born to women captives who were then murdered to be brought up by military families.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> under <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>’s 1939–75 dictatorship the newborns of some left-wing opponents of the regime, or unmarried or poor couples, were removed from their mothers and adopted. New mothers were frequently told their babies had died suddenly after birth and the hospital had taken care of their burials, when in fact they were given or sold to another family. It is believed that up to 300,000 babies were involved. These system – which allegedly involved <a href="/wiki/Controversies_about_Opus_Dei#Alleged_baby_selling_business" title="Controversies about Opus Dei">Opus Dei</a>, a vast network of doctors, nurses, nuns and priests – outlived Franco’s death in 1975 and carried on as an illegal baby trafficking network until 1987 when a new law regulating adoption was introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption_terminology">Adoption terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Adoption terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Language_of_adoption" title="Language of adoption">Language of adoption</a></div>
<p>The <b>language of adoption</b> is changing and evolving, and since the 1970s has been a controversial issue tied closely to adoption reform efforts. The controversy arises over the use of terms which, while designed to be more appealing or less offensive to some persons affected by adoption, may simultaneously cause offense or insult to others. This controversy illustrates the problems in adoption, as well as the fact that coining new words and phrases to describe ancient social practices will not necessarily alter the feelings and experiences of those affected by them. Two of the contrasting sets of terms are commonly referred to as <b>positive adoption language</b> (PAL) (sometimes called <i>respectful adoption language (RAL)</i>), and <b>honest adoption language</b> (HAL).
</p>
<h4><span id="Positive_adoptive_language_.28PAL.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_adoptive_language_(PAL)">Positive adoptive language (PAL)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Positive adoptive language (PAL)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 1970s, as adoption search and support organizations developed, there were challenges to the language in common use at the time. As books like <i>Adoption Triangle</i> by Sorosky, Pannor and Baran were published, and support groups formed like CUB (Concerned United Birthparents), a major shift from "natural parent" to "birthparent"<sup id="cite_ref-Birthparent_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birthparent-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> occurred. Along with the change in times and social attitudes came additional examination of the language used in adoption.
</p><p>Social workers and other professionals in the field of adoption began changing terms of use to reflect what was being expressed by the parties involved. In 1979, Marietta Spencer wrote "The Terminology of Adoption" for The Child Welfare League of America (CWLA),<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> which was the basis for her later work "Constructive Adoption Terminology".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> This influenced Pat Johnston's "Positive Adoption Language" (PAL) and "Respectful Adoption Language" (RAL).<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>  The terms contained in "Positive Adoption Language" include the terms "birth mother" (to replace the terms "natural mother" and "real mother"), and "placing" (to replace the term "surrender"). These kinds of recommendations encouraged people to be more aware of their use of adoption terminology.
</p>
<h4><span id="Honest_adoption_language_.28HAL.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Honest_adoption_language_(HAL)">Honest adoption language (HAL)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Honest adoption language (HAL)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>"Honest Adoption Language" refers to a set of terms that proponents say reflect the point of view that: (1) family relationships (social, emotional, psychological or physical) that existed prior to the legal adoption often continue past this point or endure in some form despite long periods of separation, and that (2) mothers who have "voluntarily surrendered" children to adoption (as opposed to involuntary terminations through court-authorized child-welfare proceedings) seldom view it as a choice that was freely made, but instead describe scenarios of powerlessness, lack of resources, and overall lack of choice.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> It also reflects the point of view that the term "birth mother" is derogatory in implying that the woman has ceased being a mother after the physical act of giving birth. Proponents of HAL liken this to the mother being treated as a "breeder" or "incubator".<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Terms included in HAL include terms that were used before PAL, including "natural mother," "first mother," and "surrendered for adoption."
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclusive_adoption_language">Inclusive adoption language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Inclusive adoption language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are supporters of various lists, developed over many decades, and there are persons who find them lacking, created to support an agenda, or furthering division. All terminology can be used to demean or diminish, uplift or embrace. In addressing the linguistic problem of naming, <a href="/wiki/Edna_Andrews" title="Edna Andrews">Edna Andrews</a> says that using "inclusive" and "neutral" language is based upon the concept that "language represents thought, and may even control thought."<sup id="cite_ref-cs_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Advocates of <a href="/wiki/Inclusive_language" title="Inclusive language">inclusive language</a> defend it as inoffensive-language usage whose goal is multi-fold:
</p>
<ol><li>The rights, opportunities, and freedoms of certain people are restricted because they are reduced to <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotypes</a>.</li>
<li>Stereotyping is mostly implicit, unconscious, and facilitated by the availability of pejorative labels and terms.</li>
<li>Rendering the labels and terms socially unacceptable, people then must consciously think about <i>how</i> they describe someone unlike themselves.</li>
<li>When labeling is a conscious activity, the described person's <i>individual</i> merits become apparent, rather than his or her stereotype.</li></ol>
<p>A common problem is that terms chosen by an identity group, as acceptable descriptors of themselves, can be used in negative ways by detractors. This compromises the integrity of the language and turns what was intended to be positive into negative or vice versa, thus often devaluing acceptability, meaning and use.
</p><p>Language at its best honors the self-referencing choices of the persons involved, uses inclusive terms and phrases, and is sensitive to the feelings of the primary parties. Language evolves with social attitudes and experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Same-sex_adoption_controversies">Same-sex adoption controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Same-sex adoption controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Charities_USA#Controversies" title="Catholic Charities USA">Catholic Charities USA §&#160;Controversies</a></div>
<p>Catholic foster and adoption agencies have been criticized for not placing children with adults perceived to be living an immoral lifestyle in Catholic theology.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_variations">Cultural variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Cultural variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_variations_in_adoption" title="Cultural variations in adoption">Cultural variations in adoption</a></div>
<p>Attitudes and laws regarding adoption vary greatly. Whereas all cultures make arrangements whereby children whose birth parents are unavailable to rear them can be brought up by others, not all cultures have the concept of adoption, that is treating unrelated children as equivalent to biological children of the adoptive parents. Under Islamic Law, for example, adopted children must keep their original surname to be identified with blood relations,<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> and, traditionally, women wear a <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a> in the presence of males in their adoptive households. In Egypt, these cultural distinctions have led to making adoption illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption_as_a_human_right">Adoption as a human right</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Adoption as a human right">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As a reaction against the bans and hurdles affecting international adoption, scholars <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Bartholet" title="Elizabeth Bartholet">Elizabeth Bartholet</a> and Paulo Barrozo claim that every child has a right to a family as a matter of basic human rights.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This claim devalues heritage or "cultural" claims and emphasizes the child's existence as a human being rather than a "property" of specific nations or, for example, abusive caregivers.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Homecoming_Day">Homecoming Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Homecoming Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In some countries, such as the United States, "Homecoming Day" is the day when an adoptee is officially united with their new adoptive family.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> In some adoptive families, this day marks an especially important event and is celebrated annually from thereafter. The term <a href="/wiki/Gotcha_Day" title="Gotcha Day">Gotcha Day</a> is also used to refer to this day.  Many adopted people and birth parents find this term to be offensive.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adoption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>Argent, Hedi. <i>Related by Adoption: a handbook for grandparents and other relatives</i> (2014)</li>
<li>Askeland, Lori. <i>Children and Youth in Adoption, Orphanages, and Foster Care: A Historical Handbook and Guide</i> (2005) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/Children-Youth-Adoption-Orphanages-Foster/dp/0313331839/">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Carp, E. Wayne, ed. <i>Adoption in America: Historical Perspectives</i> (2002)</li>
<li>Carp, E. Wayne. <i>Family Matters: Secrecy and Disclosure in the History of Adoption</i> (2000)</li>
<li>Carp, E. Wayne. <i>Jean Paton and the Struggle to Reform American Adoption</i> (University of Michigan Press; 2014) 422 pages; Scholarly biography of an activist (1908–2002) who led the struggle for open adoption records</li>
<li>Conn, Peter. <i>Adoption: A Brief Social and Cultural History</i>  (2013) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/Adoption-Social-Cultural-History-ebook/dp/B00B5SERPA/">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Eskin, Michael. <i>The Wisdom of Parenthood: An Essay</i> (New York: Upper West Side Philosophers, Inc. 2013)</li>
<li>Fessler, Ann. <i>The Girls Who Went Away: The Hidden History of Women Who Surrendered Children for Adoption in the Decades Before Roe v. Wade</i> (2007) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/The-Girls-Who-Went-Away-ebook/dp/B008RMF4GS/">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Gailey, Christine Ward. <i>Blue-Ribbon Babies and Labors of Love: Race, Class, and Gender in U.S. Adoption Practice</i> (University of Texas Press; 185 pages; 2010). Uses interviews with 131 adoptive parents in a study of how adopters' attitudes uphold, accommodate, or subvert prevailing ideologies of kinship in the United States.</li>
<li>Melosh, Barbara. <i>Strangers and Kin: the American Way of Adoption</i> (2002) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/Strangers-Kin-American-Adoption-ebook/dp/B002RHN9AC/">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Minchella, Tina Danielle. <i>Adoption in post-Soviet Russia: Nationalism and the re-invention of the "Russian family"</i> (2011)</li>
<li>Pertman, A. (2000). <i>Adoption Nation: How the Adoption Revolution Is Transforming America</i>. New York: Basic Books.</li>
<li>Seligmann, Linda J. <i>Broken Links, Enduring Ties: American Adoption Across Race, Class, and Nation</i>  (Stanford University Press; 2013) 336 pages); comparative ethnographic study of transnational and interracial adoption.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://static1.1.sqspcdn.com/static/f/384896/15481602/1323212200077/Gibson_FictiveKinship-Making-Maladaptation-Palatable.pdf?token=eX3H3pqQsaF451kHtE743fsUQaw%3D">Fictive Kinship: Making Maladaptation Palatable</a></li></ul>
